                        document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
                    }
                }
            }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Biotechnology 2013 31:3"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2013-01-27"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="A draft sequence of the staple crop kabuli chickpea, together with resequencing and analysis of 90 additional lines from 10 countries, provides a resource for breeders. Chickpea (Cicer arietinum) is the second most widely grown legume crop after soybean, accounting for a substantial proportion of human dietary nitrogen intake and playing a crucial role in food security in developing countries. We report the &#8764;738-Mb draft whole genome shotgun sequence of CDC Frontier, a kabuli chickpea variety, which contains an estimated 28,269 genes. Resequencing and analysis of 90 cultivated and wild genotypes from ten countries identifies targets of both breeding-associated genetic sweeps and breeding-associated balancing selection. Candidate genes for disease resistance and agronomic traits are highlighted, including traits that distinguish the two main market classes of cultivated chickpea&#8212;desi and kabuli. These data comprise a resource for chickpea improvement through molecular breeding and provide insights into both genome diversity and domestication."/>

itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Gengyun-Zhang" data-author-popup="auth-Gengyun-Zhang">Gengyun Zhang</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff3">3</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" Shenzhen, China" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" Singh</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff13">13</a>,<a href="#Aff21">21</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization"             <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>Chickpea (<i>Cicer arietinum</i>) is the second most widely grown legume crop after soybean, accounting for a substantial proportion of human dietary nitrogen intake and playing a crucial role in food security in developing countries. We report the <span class="stix">∼</span>738-Mb draft whole genome shotgun sequence of CDC Frontier, a <i>kabuli</i> chickpea variety, which contains an estimated 28,269 genes. Resequencing and analysis of 90 cultivated and wild genotypes from ten countries identifies targets of both breeding-associated genetic sweeps and breeding-associated balancing selection. Candidate genes for disease resistance and agronomic traits are highlighted, including traits that distinguish the two main market classes of cultivated chickpea—<i>desi</i> and <i>kabuli</i>. These data comprise a resource for chickpea improvement through molecular breeding and provide insights into both genome diversity and domestication.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>
                
                    <div class="c-pdf-button__container">

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The staple crop chickpea (<i>Cicer arietinum</i>) (2<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> = 16) is the world's second most widely grown legume. Its cultivation is of particular importance to food security in the developing world where, owing to its capacity for symbiotic nitrogen fixation, chickpea seeds are a primary source of human dietary protein<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Jukanti, A.K., Gaur, P.M., Gowda, C.L. &amp; Chibbar, R.N. Nutritional quality and health benefits of chickpea (Cicer arietinum L.): a review. Br. J. Nutr. 108, S11–S26 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d38476e1251">1</a></sup>. Chickpea is a member of the Papilionoid subfamily of legumes, a clade that contains essentially all of the important legume crops. Within this subfamily, chickpea is most closely related to crops such as alfalfa (<i>Medicago sativa</i>), clover (<i>Trifolium</i> spp.), pea (<i>Pisum sativum</i>), lentil (<i>Lens culinaris)</i>, and the model legumes barrel medic (<i>Medicago truncatula</i>) and <i>Lotus japonicus</i>. Soybean (<i>Glycine max</i>) and its allied species are more distant relations of chickpea. Originating in southeast Turkey and Syria, chickpea was one of the founder crops of modern agriculture<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="van der Maesen, L.J.G. Origin, history and taxonomy of chickpea in The Chickpea (eds. Saxena, M.C. &amp; Singh, K.B.) 11–34 (C.A.B. International, 1987)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d38476e1278">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Zohary, D. &amp; Hopf, M. Domestication of pulses in the old world: legumes were companions of wheat and barley when agriculture began in the Near East. Science 182, 887–894 (1973)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d38476e1281">3</a></sup>. There are two main types of chickpeas: small-seeded <i>desi</i> and larger-seeded <i>kabuli</i>. Consumption of <i>desi</i> is restricted primarily to the Middle East and Southeast Asia, whereas <i>kabuli</i> is a popular and valuable global commodity.</p><p>In common with many other widely grown crops, chickpea has a narrow genetic base that has resulted from domestication. Recent breeding efforts over the past 60 years have been restricted to the limited introduction of diverse germplasm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Varshney, R.K., Thudi, M., May, G.D. &amp; Jackson, S.A. Legume genomics and breeding. Plant Breed. Rev. 33, 257–304 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d38476e1300">4</a></sup>. In much of the world, chickpea is cultivated in semi-arid environments and on soils of poor agricultural quality, which, combined with its susceptibility to drought and debilitating fungal diseases, have restricted yields to &lt;1 ton/ha, which is considerably below the theoretical potential. Genetic improvement, either by traditional or molecular methods, has been hampered by the limited genomic resources coupled with narrow genetic diversity in the elite gene pool<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Varshney, R.K., Thudi, M., May, G.D. &amp; Jackson, S.A. Legume genomics and breeding. Plant Breed. Rev. 33, 257–304 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d38476e1304">4</a></sup>.</p><p>Here we report the draft whole genome shotgun (WGS) sequence of the genotype CDC Frontier, a Canadian <i>kabuli</i> chickpea variety (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). This inbred line is widely cultivated and is resistant to several important fungal diseases, including <i>Ascochyta</i> blight, and insects like pod borer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Warkentin, T. et al. CDC Frontier kabuli chickpea. Can. J. Plant Sci. 85, 909–910 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d38476e1320">5</a></sup>. To understand the genetic history among chickpea accessions, we resequenced 29 elite varieties from both <i>desi</i> and <i>kabuli</i> genotypes grown around the world, and we conducted genotyping by sequencing of 61 <i>Cicer</i> accessions from ten countries (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>We obtained 153.01 Gb of sequence data, representing 207.32× genome coverage, by Illumina sequencing of 11 genomic libraries with insert sizes ranging from 180 bp to 20 kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 2</a>). After filtering, 87.65 Gb of high-quality sequence data were assembled into 544.73 Mb of genomic sequence scaffolds with 50% of all bases in scaffolds larger than 645.3 kb (N50) and a maximum of 6.17 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 3)</a>. Based on <i>k</i>-mer statistics, the chickpea genome is estimated to be 738.09 Mb in size (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>), which indicates that 73.8% of the genome is captured in scaffolds. The remaining 36.3% (nonassembled genome) is enriched for repetitive sequences, as suggested both by the increased read depth in repeat-containing regions compared to nonrepeat regions (161-fold versus 74-fold) and a fourfold lower <i>k</i>-mer diversity in the nonassembled fraction compared to the nonrepetitive assembled fraction.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Chickpea genome assembly, gene annotation and non-protein coding genes</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nbt.2491/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>An improved assembly, that spans 532.29 Mb (N50 = 39.99 Mb) and contains 7,163 scaffolds, was produced with the aid of 46,270 repeat-masked paired bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC) end sequences <a href="http://cicar.comparative-legumes.org/gb2/gbrowse/Ca1.0/">http://cicar.comparative-legumes.org/gb2/gbrowse/Ca1.0/</a>. We anchored 65.23% of this assembly to eight genetic linkage groups using 1,292 previously published genetic markers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Hiremath, P.J. et al. Large-scale development of cost-effective SNP marker assays for diversity assessment and genetic mapping in chickpea and comparative mapping in legumes. Plant Biotechnol. J. 10, 716–732 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d38476e1930">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Thudi, M. et al. Novel SSR markers from BAC-end sequences, DArT arrays and a comprehensive genetic map with 1,291 marker loci for chickpea (Cicer arietinum L.). PLoS ONE 6, e27275 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d38476e1933">7</a></sup>. We used the combined data to identify eight pseudomolecules, Ca1 to Ca8 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 5</a>). The placement of 93.4% of these scaffolds was verified using 5,953 polymorphic restriction site–associated DNA (RAD) single-nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) markers that were analyzed in two segregating recombinant inbred line populations. Among the mapped scaffolds/contigs, 75% contained a minimum of 3 SNPs (an average of 15 SNPs/scaffold), which enabled validation of scaffold structure based on the coherence of genotype calls. Using this approach, we identified a low proportion of chimeric scaffolds (1.7% of total scaffolds) that contained 4.6 Mbp of misassembled sequence. These chimeric scaffolds were split and the erroneous portion of scaffold sequences were removed from the pseudomolecule models. We also included in the pseudomolecules 18 scaffolds that contained 4.6 Mb of sequence (0.8% of the assembly) that lack genetic support, but for which the <i>M</i>. <i>truncatula</i> genome predicts precise locations based on conserved synteny. These synteny-based placements are hypothetical regions within the pseudomolecules that will be updated as additional genetic data become available for chickpea, or if the <i>M. truncatula</i> genome assembly is modified. The RAD genotyping data were sufficient to orient 75% of scaffolds. We used comparisons to <i>M. truncatula</i> to presumptively orient the remaining 25% of scaffolds.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="The chickpea genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: The chickpea genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2491/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2491/MediaObjects/41587_2013_Article_BFnbt2491_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2491/MediaObjects/41587_2013_Article_BFnbt2491_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="689"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Pseudomolecules (A), gene density (red, B); repeat density (blue, C); retrotransposon density (violet, D); transposon density (magenta, E); average pair-wise nucleotide diversity (theta pi) across 17 <i>desi</i> chickpea varieties (green, F), 12 <i>kabuli</i> chickpea varieties (orange, G) and 29 leading chickpea varieties (black, H).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2491/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Tandem Repeat Finder was used to identify 127,377 regions of tandem repeats in the assembly. We found that 84.9% of repeats occur in tracts of &lt;1 kb (average 300 bp), whereas analysis of gap-spanning clones revealed that 0.8% of repeat regions are predicted to be from tracts of 10–103 kb. We could not assemble 29,018 repetitive regions (32.77 Mb in total) owing to low sequence complexity, and in these cases the occurrence of repeats was masked by the insertion of NNs within the pseudomolecules (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 6</a>).</p><p>Using a combination of <i>ab initio</i> modeling, and homology-based searches with gene sets from six plant species, including legumes, and the CaTA transcript sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Hiremath, P.J. et al. Large-scale transcriptome analysis in chickpea (Cicer arietinum L.), an orphan legume crop of the semi-arid tropics of Asia and Africa. Plant Biotechnol. J. 9, 922–931 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d38476e1994">8</a></sup>, we predicted a nonredundant set of 28,269 gene models, with average transcript and coding sequence sizes of 3,055 bp and 1,166 bp, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 7</a>). Most of these genes have homology with gene models in TrEMBL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Bairoch, A. &amp; Apweiler, R. The SWISS-PROT protein sequence database and its supplement TrEMBL in 2000. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 45–48 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2001">9</a></sup> (89.58%) and Interpro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Zdobnov, E.M. &amp; Apweiler, R. InterProScan–an integration platform for the signature recognition methods in InterPro. Bioinformatics 17, 847–848 (2001)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2005">10</a></sup> (70.03%) databases. Functions were tentatively assigned to 25,365 (89.73%) of genes with 2,904 genes (10.27%) unannotated (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 8</a>). As expected, gene density increases toward the ends of the pseudomolecules (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). For nonprotein coding genes, we predict 684 tRNA, 478 rRNA, 420 microRNA (miRNA) and 647 small nuclear RNA (snRNA) genes in the genome assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 9</a>).</p><p>To assess the proportion of the gene space captured in this draft genome assembly, we mapped a 454/Roche transcriptome data set (&gt;500 bp read length), produced from the same CDC Frontier line and comprising 60,802 reads, to the genome assembly. On the basis of this analysis, we estimate gene coverage to be 90.8% (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 10</a>). Analysis of the draft genome assembly for core eukaryotic genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061–1067 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2026">11</a></sup> reveals homologs for &gt;98% of conserved genes in the assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 11</a>). To evaluate the conservation of chickpea gene models in other plant species, we used BLASTP to query the chickpea proteome against the proteomes of <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>M. truncatula</i>, <i>G. max</i>, <i>Cajanus cajan</i> and <i>L. japonicus</i>. Using this analysis, proteins predicted for chickpea were most similar to those from <i>M. truncatula</i> (89.7% of predicted chickpea proteins were similar to <i>M. truncatula</i> proteins) and least similar to those from <i>A. thaliana</i> (79.2% had similarity with <i>A. thaliana</i> proteins) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 12</a>). We also observed five instances in which organelle genome segments of &gt;10 kb had been integrated into chickpea pseudomolecules, consistent with findings in both plant and animal genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Timmis, J.N. et al. Endosymbiotic gene transfer: organelle genomes forge eukaryotic chromosomes. Nat. Rev. Genet. 5, 123–135 (2004)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2065">12</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome organization and evolution</h3><p>Approximately half (49.41%) of the chickpea genome is composed of transposable elements and unclassified repeats (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>), which is comparable to other sequenced legumes: <i>M. truncatula</i> (30.5%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Young, N.D. et al. The Medicago genome provides insight into the evolution of rhizobial symbioses. Nature 480, 520–524 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2084">13</a></sup>, pigeonpea (<i>C. cajan</i>, 51.6%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Varshney, R.K. et al. Draft genome sequence of pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan), an orphan legume crop of resource-poor farmers. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 83–89 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2091">14</a></sup> and soybean (59%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178–183 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2096">15</a></sup>. Long-terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons are the most abundant transposable element class, and cover &gt;45% of the total nuclear genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#Tab2">Table 2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Organization of repetitive sequences in the chickpea genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nbt.2491/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Centromere regions are composed of microsatellites that are arranged as tandem repeats. The most abundant tandem repeats in the genome are 163-bp, 100-bp and 74-bp unit repeats, and account for 18%, 30% and 13% of identified tandem repeats, respectively. The 163-bp and 100-bp repeats are similar to the previously identified chickpea microsatellites, <i>CaSat1</i> and <i>CaSat2</i>, respectively, whereas the 74-bp repeat is homologous to <i>CaRep2</i>, a dispersed highly repetitive element<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Staginnus, C., Winter, P., Desel, C., Schmidt, T. &amp; Kahl, G. Molecular structure and chromosomal localization of major repetitive DNA families in the chickpea (Cicer arietinum L.) genome. Plant Mol. Biol. 39, 1037–1050 (1999)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2536">16</a></sup>. The 74-bp tandem repeats were organized 'head to tail' with multiple copies within a previously identified LTR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Staginnus, C., Winter, P., Desel, C., Schmidt, T. &amp; Kahl, G. Molecular structure and chromosomal localization of major repetitive DNA families in the chickpea (Cicer arietinum L.) genome. Plant Mol. Biol. 39, 1037–1050 (1999)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2540">16</a></sup> that we conclude was misannotated. The fluorescence <i>in situ</i> hybridization (FISH) experiments with oligonucleotide probes for the 100-bp tandem repeat revealed centromeric and pericentromeric distribution on pro-metaphase chromosomes in agreement with previous reports<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Staginnus, C., Winter, P., Desel, C., Schmidt, T. &amp; Kahl, G. Molecular structure and chromosomal localization of major repetitive DNA families in the chickpea (Cicer arietinum L.) genome. Plant Mol. Biol. 39, 1037–1050 (1999)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2548">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Zatloukalova, P. et al. Integration of genetic and physical maps of the chickpea (Cicer arietinum L.) genome using flow-sorted chromosomes. Chromosome Res. 19, 729–739 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2551">17</a></sup>. The 74-bp tandem repeats are distributed along all chromosomes but excluded from regions containing the 100-bp tandem repeat (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>).</p><p>Analysis of the genome sequence for segmental duplications provided evidence for 110 syntenic blocks that ranged in size from 5 to 62 gene pairs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 13)</a>. The rates of synonymous substitution per synonymous site (Ks) in these blocks indicate a divergence time of 58 million years (Myr) ago, consistent with the genome duplication event that occurred at the base of the Papilionoideae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Cannon, S.B. et al. Polyploidy did not predate the evolution of nodulation in all legumes. PLoS ONE 5, e11630 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2564">18</a></sup>. In this family, the galegoid (<i>M. truncatula</i>, <i>L. japonicus</i> and chickpea) and millettioid (soybean, pigeonpea) clades separated <span class="stix">∼</span>54 Myr ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Lavin, M., Herendeen, P.S. &amp; Wojciechowski, M.F. Evolutionary rates analysis of Leguminosae implicates a rapid diversification of lineages during the tertiary. Syst. Biol. 54, 575–594 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2574">19</a></sup>. Genome analysis of the galegoid species using genetic distance–transversion rates at fourfold degenerate sites (4DTV) revealed that chickpea diverged from <i>L. japonicus</i> <span class="stix">∼</span>20–30 Myr ago and from <i>M</i>. <i>truncatula</i> <span class="stix">∼</span>10–20 Myr ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Comparison of chickpea, legume and other dicotyledonous genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Comparison of chickpea, legume and other dicotyledonous genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2491/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2491/MediaObjects/41587_2013_Article_BFnbt2491_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2491/MediaObjects/41587_2013_Article_BFnbt2491_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="426"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Age distribution of 4DTV for genes from three legume species (chickpea, <i>M. truncatula</i> and <i>L. japonicus</i>) genomes. (<b>b</b>) Synteny blocks shared between chickpea and other sequenced legume genomes, including <i>M. truncatula</i>, <i>L. japonicus,</i> soybean and pigeonpea. (<b>c</b>) Shared and unique gene families in legume species chickpea, <i>M. truncatula</i>, <i>L. japonicus,</i> soybean, pigeonpea; in millettioid and galegoids, and in legumes, <i>A. thaliana</i> and grape. (<b>d</b>) Phylogenetic tree of seven species.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2491/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Examination of synteny with other legume and selected nonlegume dicot genomes revealed extensive conservation between chickpea and six other plant species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 13</a>), with 87–90% of the chickpea assembly showing evidence of conservation with one or more of these six genomes. The largest number of extended (&gt;10 kb) conserved syntenic blocks was observed for <i>M. truncatula</i>, whereas synteny with <i>L. japonicus</i> was considerably more fragmented (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). Among legumes, soybean had the highest number of synteny blocks, reflecting its recent polyploid ancestry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178–183 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2660">15</a></sup>, whereas the fractured colinearity with pigeonpea likely reflects the incomplete status of the pigeonpea genome assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Varshney, R.K. et al. Draft genome sequence of pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan), an orphan legume crop of resource-poor farmers. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 83–89 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2665">14</a></sup>.</p><p>Reciprocal pair-wise comparisons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C.J. &amp; Roos, D.S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2673">20</a></sup> of the 28,269 chickpea gene models with 230,161 gene models from four sequenced legumes (<i>M. truncatula</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Young, N.D. et al. The Medicago genome provides insight into the evolution of rhizobial symbioses. Nature 480, 520–524 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2679">13</a></sup>, <i>L. japonicus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Sato, S. et al. Genome structure of the legume, Lotus japonicus. DNA Res. 15, 227–239 (2008)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2685">21</a></sup>, pigeonpea<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Varshney, R.K. et al. Draft genome sequence of pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan), an orphan legume crop of resource-poor farmers. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 83–89 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2689">14</a></sup>, soybean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178–183 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2694">15</a></sup>) and two nonlegume species (<i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="The Arabidopsis genome initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2700">22</a></sup> and grape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Velasco, R . et al. A high quality draft consensus sequence of the genome of a heterozygous grapevine variety. PLoS ONE 2, e1326 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2704">23</a></sup>) identified 15,441 orthologous groups (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 14</a>). On the one hand, 5,940 of these orthologous groups contain only a single chickpea gene, suggestive of simple orthology (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 15</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). On the other hand, 4,468 chickpea genes occur in species-specific groups, with identifiable chickpea paralogs but lacking genes from other species; such groups might arise from structural rearrangements that obscure simple orthology (e.g., nonallelic recombination or gene conversion) followed by duplication, as occurs among nucleotide-binding site leucine-rich repeat (NBS-LRR) disease resistance genes.</p><p>Adjusting for 2,871 genes that were not classified by OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C.J. &amp; Roos, D.S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2721">20</a></sup>, a minimum of 69% of predicted chickpea genes have a history of duplication after the divergence of the legumes from <i>A. thaliana</i> and grape. This same time interval includes the whole genome duplication event at the base of the Papilionoideae so the chickpea genome has been shaped by a combination of gene loss and duplication. Interestingly, several thousand genes from each of the seven species analyzed could not be placed into orthologous groups. This might be due to heterogeneity in gene prediction in the seven species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 15</a>), but it might also reflect lineage-specific evolution.</p><p>Comparisons at higher taxonomic levels (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>) revealed 16,098 orthologous groups conserved between any two galegoid species (<i>M. truncatula, L. japonicus</i> and chickpea), 15,503 orthologous groups conserved between millettioid species (pigeonpea and soybean), and 16,380 orthologous groups derived from the galegoid-millettioid split near the base of the Papilionoideae. Similarly, 10,667 orthologous groups are common to legumes, <i>A. thaliana</i> and grape. This catalog of homologous relationships provides an important foundation for comparative biology and functional inference in chickpea, as well as other species, because genes with simple orthologous relationships often exhibit conserved functions, whereas genes duplicated recently relative to speciation often underlie functional diversification.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Disease resistance genes</h3><p>Among the largest gene families in plants are the NBS-LRR genes, which confer resistance to a broad range of plant pests and pathogens. The chickpea genome assembly contains 187 disease resistance gene homologs (RGHs), of which 153 are anchored in pseudomolecules. These numbers are considerably less than those observed in other legume species using comparable methods (e.g., <i>M. truncatula</i>, 764; soybean, 506; and pigeonpea, 406).</p><p>To explore the possibility that the low number of chickpea RGHs results from their disproportionate representation in unassembled regions of the genome, we compared nucleotide binding site (NBS) domains from CaGA v1.0 (the version 1.0 of <i>C. arietinum</i> Genome Assembly) with 132 NSB domains derived from PCR amplification of the <i>C. arietinum</i> genome. Phylogenetic analysis of these sequences, including <i>M. truncatula</i> RGHs to root sequence divergence, reveal no evidence of missing RGH loci in the genome assembly, further supporting the completeness of the assembly. Whereas 74 CaGA v1.0 RGHs reside in clades lacking PCR-derived RGHs, in no case is there a PCR-derived NBS domain that does not reside in close proximity to a representative from CaGA v1.0 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Figs. 5 and 6</a>). Furthermore, <i>C. arietinum</i> and <i>M. truncatula</i> RGHs are evenly distributed across the pseudomolecules, indicating that if there has been RGH gene loss, it is likely a gradual process rather than an acute loss of one or more major sequence clades.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Polymorphisms for chickpea breeding and genetics</h3><p>Simple sequence repeat (SSR) and SNP markers are valuable tools for molecular breeding. The chickpea genome assembly contained 81,845 SSRs, of which 48,298 SSRs were suitable for PCR primer design for use as genetic markers (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Tables 16 and 17</a>). SNP discovery using 1,073 million Illumina transcript reads and 15 million 454-sequencer transcript reads generated in an earlier study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Hiremath, P.J. et al. Large-scale transcriptome analysis in chickpea (Cicer arietinum L.), an orphan legume crop of the semi-arid tropics of Asia and Africa. Plant Biotechnol. J. 9, 922–931 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2787">8</a></sup> from four <i>desi</i> genotypes of chickpea (ICC 506, ICC 1882, ICC 4958, ICCC 37) and one accession (PI 489777) of the progenitor species <i>C. reticulatum</i> identified a total of 76,084 SNPs in 15,526 genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 18</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>). Of these gene-associated SNPs, 27,117 were present in the cultivated accessions, with a maximum of 22,505 between CDC Frontier and any one of the four cultivated <i>desi</i> genotypes. By contrast 54,178 SNPs were identified in the comparison of CDC Frontier with <i>C. reticulatum</i> PI 489777, most of which (49,957) were monomorphic among the four cultivated accessions. This result is consistent with the basal nature of <i>C. reticulatum</i> and a reduction of diversity in the cultivated gene pool. Although a portion of the cultivated SNPs may represent novel variation in cultivated germplasm, this situation can only be properly assessed through a more extensive survey of wild genotypes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Genetic diversity among cultivated varieties and germplasm</h3><p>With the objective of understanding genetic diversity in chickpea, we used whole genome resequencing (WGRS) of 29 (17 <i>desi</i> and 12 <i>kabuli</i>) chickpea breeding lines and released varieties collected from the leading chickpea-growing countries. WGRS yielded 204.52 Gb of high-quality sequence data with an average coverage of 9.5×, from which we calculated the average pairwise nucleotide diversity within population (θπ), Watterson's estimator of segregating sites (θ<sub>w</sub>) and Tajima's D, commonly used metrics of genetic diversity. Although the sample size was small, diversity in the <i>desi</i> group was slightly higher than the <i>kabuli</i> group across all pseudomolecules except Ca4 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 19</a>). These results are consistent with the fact that <i>kabuli</i> is defined primarily on the basis of traits derived after domestication including large and light colored seeds, so <i>kabuli</i> varieties have likely undergone a more recent, secondary bottleneck.</p><p>Plotting diversity metrics in sliding windows across the genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#Fig1">Figs. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#Fig3">3</a>) reveals high θπ and θw, which are usually associated with repeat-rich, gene-poor genome intervals. We noted several intervals of Tajima's D &gt; 2 or D &lt; −2, which are consistent with either recent balancing selection for diverse allele content (D &gt; 2) or selective sweeps and/or purifying selection (D &lt; −2). Regions of elevated Tajima's D encompass 4.8% of the genome, yet contain 12% of the anchored NBS-LRR disease resistance genes. NBS-LRR genes are known to be targets of diversifying selection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="McDowell, J.M. et al. Intragenic recombination and diversifying selection contribute to the evolution of downy mildew resistance at the RPP8 locus of Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 10, 1861–1874 (1998)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2854">24</a></sup>; however, the identity of possible pathogen targets in chickpea remains uncertain. Among the 0.7% of the genome with Tajima's D &lt; −2 is a region of Ca4 that contains a tandem array of three co-orthologs of the MATE family transporter TT12. The <i>M. truncatula</i> ortholog of TT12 functions in condensed tannin formation, which results in pigmented seeds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Zhao, J. &amp; Dixon, R.A. MATE transporters facilitate vacuolar uptake of epicatechin 3′-O-glucoside for proanthocyanidin biosynthesis in Medicago truncatula and Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 21, 2323–2340 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2861">25</a></sup>. This signature of purifying selection was evident in dark-colored <i>desi</i> but not light-colored <i>kabuli</i> genotypes, consistent with ongoing purifying selection for seed color. Interestingly, Ca4 is the most differentiated between <i>kabuli</i> and <i>desi</i> types and Ca4 also contains most of the mapped traits that distinguish <i>kabuli</i> from <i>desi</i> genotypes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Millan, T. et al. A consensus genetic map of chickpea (Cicer atietinum L.) based on 10 mapping populations. Euphytica 175, 175–189 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2885">26</a></sup>. Establishing precise genetic correlations and testing the biological significance of the underlying genes represent key opportunities for chickpea breeding and biotechnology.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Diversity in elite desi and kabuli chickpea varieties."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Diversity in elite <i>desi</i> and <i>kabuli</i> chickpea varieties.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2491/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2491/MediaObjects/41587_2013_Article_BFnbt2491_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2491/MediaObjects/41587_2013_Article_BFnbt2491_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1453"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Diversity metrics, presented as average pair-wise nucleotide diversity (current [θπ] and historical [θw]) and Tajima's D, are shown across all eight pseudomolecules in 29 elite (17 <i>desi</i> and 12 <i>kabuli</i>) chickpea varieties. #, six regions with increased Tajima's D and high FST are present on pseudomolecules Ca2, Ca3 and Ca4. +, pseudomolecule Ca4 has a region with reduced Tajima's D (–2.32) that conatins 11 genes including 3MATE transporter TT12 orthologs. *, NBS-LRR disease resistance genes are associated with regions of elevated Tajima's D on five pesudomolecules namely Ca1, Ca2, Ca4, Ca7 and Ca8.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2491/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To gain a genome-wide view of genetic structure, we sequenced an additional 61 genotypes using restriction site–associated DNA (RAD) protocols, which produced an additional 34.77 Gb of data. Combined with the 29 genotypes used for WGRS, the total set of 90 <i>Cicer</i> accessions includes 60 improved chickpea lines, 25 landraces, 4 accessions of <i>C. reticulatum</i> and 1 accession of <i>C. echinospermum</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>). Within this set we identified 4.4 million variants (SNPs and INDELs) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 20</a>). Principal component analysis (PCA) reflects limited genetic diversity in two distinct groups of cultivated genotypes that are mixtures of <i>desi</i> and <i>kabuli</i> types (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>, principal component 1), and a more diverse spread of wild genotypes. Analyses based either on pair-wise dissimilarity using neighbor joining (NJ) or allele frequencies using structure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Pritchard, J.K., Stephens, P. &amp; Donnelly, P. Inference of population structure using multilocus genotype data. Genetics 155, 945–959 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2950">27</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>) also revealed several distinct groups. Again, these major groups do not reflect the traditionally held separation of <i>desi</i> and <i>kabuli</i> genotypes, and this observation holds true for both advanced breeding lines and landraces. Group I contains all 5 wild-species genotypes and 8 landraces (6 <i>desi</i> and 2 <i>kabuli</i>), group II includes 31 cultivars and/or breeding lines and 17 landraces (12 <i>desi</i> and 5 <i>kabuli</i>) and group III contains all 29 elite varieties (19 <i>desi</i> and 10 <i>kabuli</i>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a>). Pedigree analysis reveals three genotypes (ICCV 96029, ICCV 94954 and ICCV 96970) that share ICCC 42 as a common parent but that were placed into two different groups (ICCV 96029 in group II and ICCV 94954 and ICCV 96970 in group III). This situation could have arisen from diverse nonrecurrent parents that have been used by breeders in developing these genotypes. Important targets for chickpea breeding are disease resistance to <i>Fusarium oxysporum</i> fsp <i>ciceri</i> and drought tolerance. Among a total of 17 <i>Fusarium</i>-resistant lines, 16 were assigned to group II, whereas three drought-tolerant genotypes namely ICC 4918, ICC 8261 and ICC 4958 were also assigned to group II. These observations suggest recurrent use by breeders of common lines to breed these valuable traits. More generally, analysis with the Structure program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Pritchard, J.K., Stephens, P. &amp; Donnelly, P. Inference of population structure using multilocus genotype data. Genetics 155, 945–959 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d38476e2995">27</a></sup> reveals numerous admixed genotypes, which highlights the mixed use of <i>desi</i> and <i>kabuli</i> genotypes in the breeding of both types of varieties. It seems that breeding has obscured the true genetic history of the <i>desi</i> and <i>kabuli</i> varieties.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Population structure and diversity in elite varieties and germplasm."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Population structure and diversity in elite varieties and germplasm.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2491/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2491/MediaObjects/41587_2013_Article_BFnbt2491_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2491/MediaObjects/41587_2013_Article_BFnbt2491_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="468"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Structure analysis of 29 elite varieties (19 <i>desi</i> and 10 <i>kabuli</i>) based on whole-genome resequencing data. (<b>b</b>) Principle component analysis (PCA) of 90 chickpea genotypes including 29 elite varieties and 61 germplasm lines using 1.96 million SNPs as datapoints. (<b>c</b>) Neighbor-joining (NJ) tree analysis of 90 genotypes based on 1.96 million SNPs.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2491/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Impact of breeding on genetic diversity</h3><p>During breeding, phenotypically and agronomically important traits are selected to develop superior varieties with improved crop productivity. As a result, genetic diversity is lost through fixation and genetic sweeps, and through breeders' increased dependence on smaller sets of superior genotypes, creating successive bottlenecks. Excluding the 5 wild species accessions, RAD-based SNP data were available for 25 landraces and 31 breeding lines or elite cultivars. After filtering for missing data, we used 4,696 high-quality segregating sites to assess genetic differentiation (that is, fixation index, F<sub>ST</sub>) between landraces and cultivars, compared using FDIST<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Beaumont, M.A. &amp; Nichols, R.A. Evaluating loci for use in the genetic analysis of population structure. Proc. R. Soc. Lond. B 263, 1619–1626 (1996)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3057">28</a></sup>. Although the data were sparse, we identified 6 genomic regions of 50–200 kb characterized by &gt;5 segregating sites from multiple RAD tags, each of which were in the top 5% of F<sub>ST</sub> values. In all cases, these genome intervals correspond to regions identified as having Tajima's D values &gt;2. Together these genome regions comprise 122 genes that are candidates for selection during modern breeding efforts, including a large set of 54 genes on Ca3 that contains a homolog of the flowering time gene CONSTANS. Furthermore, a functional flowering time quantitative trait locus is roughly mapped to the same location on Ca3 as this 54-gene set and breeding-based manipulation of flowering time has been a crucial factor in adapting elite chickpea germplasm to different agro-climatic zones.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec14" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec14-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec14">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec14-content"><p>This draft whole genome sequence of chickpea (CDC Frontier, a <i>kabuli</i> chickpea variety) adds to the genomic resources available for legume research. The Papilionoideae subfamily now has the draft or complete genome sequences of two model species (<i>M. truncatula</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Young, N.D. et al. The Medicago genome provides insight into the evolution of rhizobial symbioses. Nature 480, 520–524 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3077">13</a></sup> and <i>L. japonicus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Sato, S. et al. Genome structure of the legume, Lotus japonicus. DNA Res. 15, 227–239 (2008)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3083">21</a></sup>) and three crop legume species (chickpea, soybean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178–183 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3087">15</a></sup> and pigeonpea<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Varshney, R.K. et al. Draft genome sequence of pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan), an orphan legume crop of resource-poor farmers. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 83–89 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3092">14</a></sup>). The availability of these genome sequences should facilitate <i>de novo</i> assembly of the genomes of other important but less-studied galegoid legume crops such as pea (<i>Pisum sativum</i>), lentil (<i>Lens culinaris</i>) and faba bean (<i>Vicia faba</i>).</p><p>In addition to identifying SSRs and SNPs based on genome scanning and RNA-seq analysis, our analysis of 90 genomes reveals numerous additional chickpea genome polymorphisms including both SNPs and INDELs. These resources will assist genomics-based breeding approaches such as genotyping by sequencing, genome-wide association studies and genomic selection. Furthermore, population structure, diversity and phylogenetic analyses not only document the mixed use of <i>desi</i> and <i>kabuli</i> genotypes in breeding, but also serve to identify regions (and candidate genes) across the genome that might have been greatly affected by selection during domestication and/or breeding. Combined with knowledge of germplasm diversity and candidate gene regions, the analyses presented here should accelerate future breeding of elite cultivars. This will eventually move us closer to the goal of improving the livelihood and productivity of chickpea farmers worldwide, with particular emphasis on the resource-poor, marginal environments of sub-Saharan Africa and Southeast Asia.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec15" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec15-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec15">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec15-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Whole-genome shotgun sequencing and assembly.</h3><p>Whole-genome shotgun sequencing was performed with the HiSeq 2000 Sequencing System. A total of 11 paired-end sequencing libraries were constructed with insert sizes of <span class="stix">∼</span>170, 500, 800, 2,000, 5,000, 10,000 and 20,000 bp. In total, 153.01 Gb data were generated of paired-ends, ranging from 49 to 100 bp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 2</a>). After stringent filtering and correction steps, only 87.65 Gb data were used for <i>de novo</i> genome assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 2</a>).</p><p>The genome size was estimated using the total length of sequence reads divided by sequencing depth, as described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Varshney, R.K. et al. Draft genome sequence of pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan), an orphan legume crop of resource-poor farmers. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 83–89 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3141">14</a></sup>. SOAPdenovo was used for assembly, scaffold construction and gap closure, as described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Varshney, R.K. et al. Draft genome sequence of pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan), an orphan legume crop of resource-poor farmers. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 83–89 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3145">14</a></sup>. Sequences of genetic markers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Hiremath, P.J. et al. Large-scale development of cost-effective SNP marker assays for diversity assessment and genetic mapping in chickpea and comparative mapping in legumes. Plant Biotechnol. J. 10, 716–732 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3149">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Thudi, M. et al. Novel SSR markers from BAC-end sequences, DArT arrays and a comprehensive genetic map with 1,291 marker loci for chickpea (Cicer arietinum L.). PLoS ONE 6, e27275 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3152">7</a></sup> were placed on genomic scaffold assemblies using BLASTN from the BLAST+ package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 421 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3156">29</a></sup>, or using e-PCR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Schuler, G.D. Sequence mapping by electronic PCR. Genome Res. 7, 541–550 (1997)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3160">30</a></sup> where only flanking primers were available. For the BLAST searches, the top match was accepted, where the E-value was &lt;1e-25 and percent identity was &gt;85. For e-PCR, the best-scoring match was accepted, allowing up to three mismatches and one gap per primer. Provisional pseudomolecules were assembled based on marker order, and refined locally based on synteny with the <i>Medicago truncatula</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Young, N.D. et al. The Medicago genome provides insight into the evolution of rhizobial symbioses. Nature 480, 520–524 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3168">13</a></sup>. Synteny was computed using promer from the MUMmer 3 package (v. 3.23)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3172">31</a></sup>, with the promer–mum option for “maximum unique matches” between pseudomolecule pairs.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Assessing genome assembly and gene space.</h3><p>The genome assembly was checked for microbial contamination by searching against databases of bacterial genomes and fungal genomes using Megablast (E-value &lt; 1e-5). To check for contamination with organellar DNA, the <i>C. arietinum</i> chloroplast (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=NC_011163.1">NC_011163.1</a>) and <i>L. japonicus</i> mitochondrion (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=NC_016743.2">NC_016743.2</a>) genomes were screened against the chickpea genome assembly using BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Kent, W.J. BLAT–the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3204">32</a></sup> (default parameters). To check the completeness of the assembly, we mapped a transcriptome assembly comprising 48,668 transcriptome assembly contigs assembled from 139,241 Sanger ESTs, 7.12 million 454/FLX and 134.95 million Illumina transcript reads, to the genome assembly using BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Kent, W.J. BLAT–the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3209">32</a></sup> at various sequence homology and coverage parameters (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 6</a>). Core eukaryotic genes identified by CEGMA v.2.3 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061–1067 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3215">11</a>) were also mapped to the genome assembly by BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Kent, W.J. BLAT–the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3219">32</a></sup> to predict exome coverage. CEGMA data were downloaded from <a href="http://korflab.ucdavis.edu/datasets/cegma/%23SCT6">http://korflab.ucdavis.edu/datasets/cegma/#SCT6</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Repeat annotation.</h3><p>There are two main types of repeats in the genome: tandem and interspersed. Tandem Repeats Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573–580 (1999)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3238">33</a></sup> was applied to the genome assembly, filtering for &gt;3 copies and &gt;60-bp consensus length. Tandem repeat abundance was estimated using counts of all unique 25-mer sequences in the genome. 25-mer sequences occurring &gt;3,000 times were used for BLAST searches to identify abundant repeats. FISH with 100-bp and 74-bp tandem repeat probes was undertaken to study repeat distribution in the genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>).</p><p>Transposable elements in the genome assembly were identified using a combination of <i>de novo</i> and homology-based approaches. Three <i>de novo</i> software programs with default parameters were used, with a minimum repeat length of 50 bp (LTR_Finder v 1.03 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265–W268 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3253">34</a>), PILER-DF v1.0 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Edgar, R.C. &amp; Myers, E.W. PILER: identification and classification of genomic repeats. Bioinformatics 21, i152–i158 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3256">35</a>) and RepeatScout v 1.05 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Price, A.L., Jones, N.C. &amp; Pevzner, P.A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21, i351–i358 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3259">36</a>) to build a chickpea repeat database. We then used RepeatMasker v 3.2.7 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Prot. Bioinform. 25, 4.10.1–4.10.14 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3263">37</a>) to identify repeats using both the chickpea repeat database and Repbase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462–467 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3267">38</a></sup>. Additionally, RepeatProteinMask (<a href="http://repeatmasker.org/">http://repeatmasker.org/</a>, v 3.2.2) was used to search the protein database in Repbase against the genome to identify repeat-related proteins. Identified repeats were classified into known repeat classes using standard methods<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Varshney, R.K. et al. Draft genome sequence of pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan), an orphan legume crop of resource-poor farmers. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 83–89 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3278">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178–183 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3281">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Price, A.L., Jones, N.C. &amp; Pevzner, P.A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21, i351–i358 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3284">36</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Gene prediction and function analysis.</h3><p>We used three approaches for gene prediction: homology-based (H), <i>de novo</i> (P) and transcript sequences-based (C). These results were integrated by GLEAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Elsik, C.G. et al. Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol. 8, R13 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3299">39</a></sup>, filtered multiple times and then checked manually (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2491#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 6</a>). This resulted in a gene set (GD-set) comprising 28,256 genes. Additionally, we identified a set of 453 core genes that are supposed to be highly conserved in all eukaryotes, using CEGMA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061–1067 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3306">11</a></sup>. Based on this analysis, a set of 13 genes out of 453 core genes did not align with any gene defined in the initial GD-set, but were in the genome sequence, so were added to the GD-set. The final “Official Gene Set” (OGSv1.0) set contains 28,269 genes.</p><p>Gene functions were assigned according to the best match of the alignments using BLASTP (E-value: 1e-5) to the SwissProt and TrEMBL databases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Bairoch, A. &amp; Apweiler, R. The SWISS-PROT protein sequence database and its supplement TrEMBL in 2000. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 45–48 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3313">9</a></sup> (release-2012_03, <a href="http://www.uniprot.org/downloads">http://www.uniprot.org/downloads</a>). InterProScan v4.7 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Zdobnov, E.M. &amp; Apweiler, R. InterProScan–an integration platform for the signature recognition methods in InterPro. Bioinformatics 17, 847–848 (2001)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3323">10</a>) determined motifs and domains of genes against protein databases including Pfam, PRINTS, PROSITE, ProDom, SMART and PANTHER. Gene Ontology IDs for each gene were obtained from the corresponding InterPro entry. All genes were aligned against KEGG (KEGG_release58) proteins using BLASTP (E-value: 1e-5), and the pathway in which the gene might be involved was derived from the matching genes in KEGG. The tRNA genes were predicted by tRNAscan-s.e.m. v1.23 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Lowe, T.M. &amp; Eddy, S.R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955–964 (1997)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3326">40</a>) with eukaryote parameters. Aligning the rRNA template sequences from plants (e.g., <i>Arabidopsis</i> and rice) using BLASTN with E-value 1e-5 identified the rRNA fragments. The miRNA and snRNA genes were predicted by INFERNAL v0.81 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Nawrocki, E.P., Kolbe, D.L. &amp; Eddy, S.R. Infernal 1.0: inference of RNA alignments. Bioinformatics 25, 1335–1337 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3333">41</a>) software against the Rfam database (release 9.1).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Analysis of orthologous genes.</h3><p>All the predicted protein sequences from chickpea, <i>Medicago</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Young, N.D. et al. The Medicago genome provides insight into the evolution of rhizobial symbioses. Nature 480, 520–524 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3348">13</a></sup>, <i>Lotus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Sato, S. et al. Genome structure of the legume, Lotus japonicus. DNA Res. 15, 227–239 (2008)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3354">21</a></sup>, soybean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178–183 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2491#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d38476e3358">15</a></sup> and pigeonpea<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Varshney, R.K. et al. Draft genome sequence of pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan), an orphan legume crop of resource-poor farmers. Nat.
